Yes, several times. As a Romanian, at home I had access to, for example, free live football games streamed on a sports TV channel's website. However, when studying in Belgium the website would reject me on a territorial base, which is unacceptable since I still payed my taxes in my home country. And useless since there are many ways to bypass this artificial control, such as using a proxy server or connecting to live streams not supplied by the official channel. This is a lose-lose situation, because the consumer loses precious time trying to find alternatives to watch a show or football match, while the TV company loses him or her as a client (since he or she uses other sites to connect to the same material) and also commercial revenue. There isalso the problem of (mostly) American movie series, that are freely available to a customer with an IP from the US, but not to a European customer. Again, the problem can be solved by the user by using alternative live streams, with the risks of getting unsolicited adware, malware and seeing the video in a poorer quality. The justification of US copyright holders could be that the commercials displayed during the video wouldn't reach their target in Europe, but that can be solved by linking the commercialswith the home IP address, something that YouTube has been doing for a while now.

This is because of a lasting conflict with the GEMA, which is a collecting society which collects money for musicians and distributes it again back to them with complex schemes. This GEMA is not happy with Googles policy of not giving them the cut of the money they feel they deserve, only to distribute it back to the same rights holders which uploaded the videos in the first place. The major problem with this is, that basically in Germany everything playing music can possibly get banned, even if the music is original and not registered with the GEMA, such as Amanda Palmers work (repeatedly), or the person bought all the necessary rights, such as with Indy film "Sita sings the Blues" ( https://www.techdirt.com/articles/20110714/00141315084/why-sita-sings-blues-is-perfectly-legal-germany-you-still-cant-watch-it-youtube.shtml ). The overarching problem in this generation seems to be, that often the burden of proof, that a work is breaching copyright or, as in these instances, is part of the GEMA domain, is not anymore put on those who claim it. They have the power to vastly just preemptively block thousands of videos, where individual people have to fight big corporations partly for months to show they legitimate work. ÔÇ NO ÔÇ NO OPINION

The European Union is made of Member-States bound by national borders that often overlap common languages areas, such as Belgium that forms one country with three official languages (French, Dutch and German). And in the north of Europe there is a Sami population that share the same language (Sami) across Norway, Sweden and Finland. Border zones also host minority populations that sometimes form big communities, such as the Danish-speaking people of Schleswig-Holstein in Germany. These quick examples show that the problem encountered by many people willing to access online content in Europe is not a consumer problem but a citizen problem rooted in European history. One of the missions of libraries as public institutions is to provide users with access to content in their own languages. This was not a problem in the printing environment, but it has become a problem in the online environment. For instance Swedish citizens duly registered in the public library of Helsing√∏r (Elsinore), Denmark can borrow physical documents (books, DVD‚Äôs, Cd‚Äôs) and go back home in Sweden and enjoy them. This is impossible for online content (e-books, movies‚Ä¶). To the detriment of users, online service provision is being hampered by copyright rules.

Yes. Typically a large amount of material is not available due to copyright claims. Different EU countries have different access levels, and this complicates the internet, and European society at large. Different access between Germany and the United Kingdom means European citizens traveling between the two countries are blocked from access. Friends across borders cannot share the same material due to different laws. The copyright holders abuse their position to fragment the entire internet "market". Different laws and different levels of access create confusion and uncertainty for all citizens, and fragment the Internet across country lines, leading to an experience of discrimination by country. In each case, this depends on the per country power of the copyright lobby.

Of course this goes against the existence of Youtube as it is, watching videos from around the clock. This doesn't only give bad reputation to Youtube, the company which owns the copyright and unsatisfaction as a user of the service, but the same company goes against itself too. Videos are not only "just sharing" the context of it, it is also a a product of a marketing medium (people can gain money for video views and advertising). So, prohibiting me from access to a certain video, also prohibits me from that market. That constitutes a logical fallacy. For example, a music video for a song from the new albumof the band "X" is uploaded to Youtube from one of their fans. The company that regulates the copyright of that band sees that there is an infringement for that video, so it reports it to Youtube and the video "goes down". Now, Joe, a user of Youtube, just learned about X, so he searches for it to Youtube. Joe has lower possibilities to listen to their new song because of the copyright infringement. As a result, Joe has a low possibility of becoming a buyer of X's new album. (note: Country of residence: Greece) ÔÇ NO ÔÇ NO OPINION

The Internet should provide equal access to everyone. It's stupid and can be trivially bypassed. Bypassing is another concern. Is using a proxy to view such material a copyright infringement? If so, why? Proxies are not illegal, they are almost fundamental for the Internet to function properly. So it shouldn't ever be a copyright infringement to use them. Either make it so, that this kind of content blocking is uniform for entire EU, or just do away with it entirely.

I frequently travel between Belgium and Germany. When in Germany I notice certain YouTube videos are not accessible when browsing under a German IP-address. I am not clear about where exactly the law on copyright between both countries differs, only that public opinion is that Germany is explicitly strict on enforcing their copyright laws. Official information on this difference is vague and often misleading. Furthermore I feel German internet policy rather aggressively limits my browsing-behavior based on IP-address, e.g. redirecting all websites to German equivalent pages, or just blocking content altogether. It should be possible to bypass this somehow, but then again this is probably mostly website-specific.

Furthermore, pictures of some buildings are not available in all language versions of Wikipedia, also due to the legal reality the delegation to the Member State level has caused.
